  "Title: Handling audio interruptions | Apple Developer Documentation\nObserve audio session notifications to ensure that your app responds appropriately to interruptions.\nOVERVIEW [about:/documentation/avfaudio/handling_audio_interruptions#overview]$zoo1Interruptions are a common part of the iOS and watchOS user experiences. For example, consider the scenario of receiving a phone\ncall while you’re watching a movie in the TV app on your iPhone. In this case, the movie’s audio fades out, playback pauses, and\nthe sound of the call’s ringtone fades in. If you decline the call, control returns to the TV app, and playback begins again as\nthe movie’s audio fades in.$zoo1At the center of this behavior is your app’s audio session. As interruptions begin and end, the audio session notifies any\nregistered observers so they can take appropriate action. For example, AVPlayer [/documentation/avfoundation/avplayer] monitors\nyour app’s audio session and automatically pauses playback in response to interruption events. You can monitor these changes by\nkey-value observing the player’s timeControlStatus [/documentation/avfoundation/avplayer/1643485-timecontrolstatus] property, and\nupdate your user interface as necessary when the player pauses and resumes playback.$zoo1CUSTOMIZE THE INTERRUPTION BEHAVIOR [about:/documentation/avfaudio/handling_audio_interruptions#3734615]$zoo1Most apps rely on the system’s default interruption behavior. However, AVAudioSession [/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession]\nprovides ways to customize the default behavior to better accommodate your app’s needs:$zoo1 * Recent iPad models provide a feature that mutes the built-in microphone at the hardware level when the user closes the device’s\n$zoo1Smart Folio cover. If your app plays and records audio, you may want to continue playback even if the system mutes the\n$zoo1microphone. You can disable the default interruption behavior by setting the overrideMutedMicrophoneInterruption\n$zoo1[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/categoryoptions/3727255-overridemutedmicrophoneinterrupt] option when configuring your\n$zoo1audio session.$zoo1 * System alerts, such as receiving an incoming phone call, interrupt the active audio session. If you prefer that the system not\n$zoo1interrupt your app’s audio session in these cases, you can indicate this preference by setting a value for the setPrefersNo\n$zoo1InterruptionsFromSystemAlerts(_:) [/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/3726094-setprefersnointerruptionsfromsys] method.$zoo1OBSERVE AUDIO SESSION INTERRUPTIONS [about:/documentation/avfaudio/handling_audio_interruptions#2948856]$zoo1You can directly observe interruption notifications that AVAudioSession posts.   "Title: Responding to audio route changes | Apple Developer Documentation\nObserve audio session notifications to ensure that your app responds appropriately to route changes.\nOVERVIEW [about:/documentation/avfaudio/responding_to_audio_route_changes#overview]$zoo1An important responsibility of AVAudioSession [/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession] is managing audio route changes. A route\nchange occurs when the system adds or removes an audio input or output. Route changes occur for several reasons, including a user\nplugging in a pair of headphones, connecting a Bluetooth LE headset, or unplugging a USB audio interface. When these changes\noccur, the audio session reroutes audio signals accordingly and broadcasts a notification containing the details of the change to\nany registered observers.$zoo1An important behavior related to route changes occurs when a user plugs in or removes a pair of headphones (see Playing audio in\nHuman Interface Guidelines [design/human-interface-guidelines]). When users connect a pair of wired or wireless headphones,\nthey’re implicitly indicating that audio playback should continue, but privately. They expect an app that’s currently playing\nmedia to continue playing without pause. However, when users disconnect their headphones, they don’t want to automatically share\nwhat they’re listening to with others. Applications should respect this implicit privacy request and automatically pause playback\nwhen users disconnect their headphones.$zoo1OBSERVE ROUTE CHANGES [about:/documentation/avfaudio/responding_to_audio_route_changes#2948863]$zoo1You can directly observe route change notifications posted by the audio session. Determine the reason for this change by retrieving the AVAudioSession.RouteChangeReason\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/routechangereason] value from the dictionary. When a user connects a new device, the\nreason is AVAudioSession.RouteChangeReason.newDeviceAvailable\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/routechangereason/newdeviceavailable], and when a user removes a device, the reason is\nAVAudioSession.RouteChangeReason.oldDeviceUnavailable\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/routechangereason/olddeviceunavailable].$zoo1When a new device becomes available, you ask the audio session for its currentRoute\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/1616453-currentroute] to determine where the audio output is currently routed. This query\nreturns an AVAudioSessionRouteDescription [/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosessionroutedescription] object that lists all of the\naudio session’s inputs and outputs. When the user removes a device, you retrieve the route description for the previous route from\nthe user-information dictionary. In both cases, you query the route description for its outputs, which returns an array of port\ndescription objects providing the details of the audio output routes.$zoo1@objc func handleRouteChange(notification: Notification) {\n$zoo1guard let userInfo = notification.userInfo,\n$zoo1let reasonValue = userInfo[AVAudioSessionRouteChangeReasonKey] as? AVAudioSessionRouteDescription {\n$zoo1headphonesConnected = hasHeadphones(in: previousRoute)\n$zoo1}\n$zoo1\n$zoo1default: ()\n$zoo1}\n}$zoo1func hasHeadphones(in routeDescription: AVAudioSessionRouteDescription) -> Bool {\n$zoo1// Filter the outputs to only those with a port type of headphones.\n$zoo1return !routeDescription.outputs.filter({$0.portType == .headphones}).isEmpty\n}",
  "Title: Capturing stereo audio from built-in microphones | Apple Developer Documentation\nConfigure an iOS device’s built-in microphones to add stereo-recording capabilities to your app.\nOVERVIEW [about:/documentation/avfaudio/capturing_stereo_audio_from_built-in_microphones#overview]$zoo1Stereo audio uses two channels to create the illusion of multidirectional sound, which adds greater depth and dimension to your\naudio and results in an immersive listening experience. iOS provides several ways to record audio from the built-in microphones,\nbut prior to iOS 14 you were limited to recording mono audio only. In iOS 14 and later, you can capture stereo audio using the\nbuilt-in microphones on supported devices.$zoo1Because a user can hold an iOS device in a variety of ways, you need to specify the orientation of the right and left channels in\nthe stereo field. You set the built-in microphone’s directionality by configuring the following:$zoo1 * Polar pattern. The system represents the individual device microphones, and beamformers that use multiple microphones, as data\n$zoo1sources. Select the front or back data source and set its polar pattern to .stereo.$zoo1 * Input orientation. When recording video, set the input orientation to match the video orientation. When recording audio only,\n$zoo1set the input orientation to match the user interface orientation. In both cases, don’t change the orientation during\n$zoo1recording.$zoo1This sample code project shows how to configure your app to record stereo audio, and helps you visualize changes to the input\norientation and data-source selection.$zoo1CONFIGURE THE AUDIO SESSION CATEGORY [about:/documentation/avfaudio/capturing_stereo_audio_from_built-in_microphones#4181780]$zoo1Recording stereo audio requires the app’s audio session to use either the record\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/category/1616451-record] or playAndRecord\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/category/1616568-playandrecord] category.   "Title: AVAudioSession | Apple Developer Documentation\nAn object that communicates to the system how you intend to use audio in your app.\nOVERVIEW [about:/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession#overview]$zoo1An audio session acts as an intermediary between your app and the operating system — and, in turn, the underlying audio hardware.\nYou use an audio session to communicate to the operating system the general nature of your app’s audio without detailing the\nspecific behavior or required interactions with the audio hardware. You delegate the management of those details to the audio\nsession, which ensures that the operating system can best manage the user’s audio experience.$zoo1All iOS, tvOS, and watchOS apps have a default audio session that comes preconfigured with the following behavior:$zoo1 * It supports audio playback, but disallows audio recording.$zoo1 * When the app plays audio, it silences any other background audio.$zoo1 * In iOS, setting the Ring/Silent switch to silent mode silences any audio the app is playing.$zoo1 * In iOS, locking a device silences the app’s audio.$zoo1Although the default audio session provides useful behavior, it generally doesn’t provide the audio behavior a media app needs. To\nchange the default behavior, you configure your app’s audio session category.$zoo1There are six possible categories you can use, but playback [/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/category/1616509-playback] is\nthe one that playback apps most commonly use. This category indicates that audio playback is a central feature of your app. When\nyou specify this category, your app’s audio continues with the Ring/Silent switch set to silent mode (iOS only). Using this\ncategory, you can also play background audio if you’re using the Audio, AirPlay, and Picture in Picture background mode. For more\ninformation, see Enabling Background Audio.$zoo1You use an AVAudioSession object to configure your app’s audio session. This class is a singleton object used to set the audio\nsession’s category, mode, and other configurations. You can interact with the audio session throughout your app’s life cycle, but\nit’s often useful to perform this configuration at app launch, as shown in the following example.$zoo1func configureAudioSession() {\n$zoo1// Retrieve the shared audio session.\n$zoo1let audioSession = AVAudioSession.sharedInstance()\n$zoo1do {\n$zoo1// Set the audio session category and mode.\n$zoo1try audioSession.setCategory(.playback, mode: .moviePlayback)\n$zoo1} catch {\n$zoo1print(\"Failed to set the audio session configuration\")\n$zoo1}\n}$zoo1The audio session uses this configuration when you activate the session using the setActive(_:)\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/1616597-setactive] or setActive(_:options:)\n[/documentation/avfaudio/avaudiosession/1616627-setactive] method.",
